<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The genome sequences of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis , the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2016 48:4"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2016-02-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2016 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="David Bertioli and colleagues report the genomes of Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis, the diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut, Arachis hypogaea. Their analyses are a first step in understanding the evolution of the peanut&#39;s tetraploid genome. Cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) is an allotetraploid with closely related subgenomes of a total size of &#8764;2.7 Gb. This makes the assembly of chromosomal pseudomolecules very challenging. As a foundation to understanding the genome of cultivated peanut, we report the genome sequences of its diploid ancestors (Arachis duranensis and Arachis ipaensis). We show that these genomes are similar to cultivated peanut&#39;s A and B subgenomes and use them to identify candidate disease resistance genes, to guide tetraploid transcript assemblies and to detect genetic exchange between cultivated peanut&#39;s subgenomes. On the basis of remarkably high DNA identity of the A. ipaensis genome and the B subgenome of cultivated peanut and biogeographic evidence, we conclude that A. ipaensis may be a direct descendant of the same population that contributed the B subgenome to cultivated peanut."/>

Isobe</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff12">12</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Kazusa DNA Research Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.410858.0, 0000 0000 9824 2470, Department of Frontier Research, Kazusa DNA Research Institute, Kisarazu, Japan" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Baozhu-Guo" data-author-popup="auth-Baozhu-Guo">Baozhu Guo</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff23">24</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Crop Protection and Management Research Unit" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.463419.d, 0000 0004 0404 0958, US Department of Agriculture–Agricultural Research Service, Crop Protection and Management Research Unit, Tifton,             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Cultivated peanut (<i>Arachis hypogaea</i>) is an allotetraploid with closely related subgenomes of a total size of <span class="stix">∼</span>2.7 Gb. This makes the assembly of chromosomal pseudomolecules very challenging. As a foundation to understanding the genome of cultivated peanut, we report the genome sequences of its diploid ancestors (<i>Arachis duranensis</i> and <i>Arachis ipaensis</i>). We show that these genomes are similar to cultivated peanut's A and B subgenomes and use them to identify candidate disease resistance genes, to guide tetraploid transcript assemblies and to detect genetic exchange between cultivated peanut's subgenomes. On the basis of remarkably high DNA identity of the <i>A. ipaensis</i> genome and the B subgenome of cultivated peanut and biogeographic evidence, we conclude that <i>A. ipaensis</i> may be a direct descendant of the same population that contributed the B subgenome to cultivated peanut.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Peanut (also called groundnut; <i>A. hypogaea</i> L.) is a grain legume and oilseed, which is widely cultivated in tropical and subtropical regions (annual production of <span class="stix">∼</span>46 million tons). It has a key role in human nutrition. In Africa and Asia, more peanut is grown than any other grain legume (including soy bean) (FAOSTAT 2015; see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Sec29">URLs</a>). The <i>Arachis</i> genus is endemic to South America and is composed mostly of diploid species (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 20). <i>A. hypogaea</i> is an allotetraploid (AABB-type genome; 2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 40), probably derived from a single recent hybridization event between two diploid species and polyploidization<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Husted, L. Cytological studies of the peanut Arachis. 2. Chromosome number, morphology and behavior, and their application to the problem of the origin of the cultivated forms. Cytologia (Tokyo) 7, 396–423 (1936)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1302">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Halward, T., Stalker, T., LaRue, E. &amp; Kochert, G. Use of single-primer DNA amplifications in genetic studies of peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.). Plant Mol. Biol. 18, 315–325 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1305">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kochert, G. et al. RFLP and cytogenetic evidence on the origin and evolution of allotetraploid domesticated peanut, Arachis hypogaea (Leguminosae). Am. J. Bot. 83, 1282–1291 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1308">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Cuc, L.M. et al. Isolation and characterization of novel microsatellite markers and their application for diversity assessment in cultivated groundnut (Arachis hypogaea). BMC Plant Biol. 8, 55 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1311">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1314">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Kochert, G., Halward, T., Branch, W.D. &amp; Simpson, C.E. RFLP variability in peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) cultivars and wild species. Theor. Appl. Genet. 81, 565–570 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1317">6</a></sup>. Chromosomes are of mostly similar size and are metacentric, but strong chromosomal centromeric banding and one pair of small chromosomes distinguish the A from the B subgenome. Cytogenetic, phylogeographic and molecular evidence indicate <i>A. duranensis</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg. and <i>A. ipaensis</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg. as the donors of the A and B subgenomes, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Kochert, G. et al. RFLP and cytogenetic evidence on the origin and evolution of allotetraploid domesticated peanut, Arachis hypogaea (Leguminosae). Am. J. Bot. 83, 1282–1291 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1328">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1331">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ramos, M.L. et al. Chromosomal and phylogenetic context for conglutin genes in Arachis based on genomic sequence. Mol. Genet. Genomics 275, 578–592 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1334">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963–1971 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1337">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relationships among the wild B genome of Arachis species (section Arachis) based on FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection: a new proposal for genome arrangement. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 1033–1046 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1340">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Robledo, G., Lavia, G.I. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relations among wild Arachis species with the A genome as revealed by FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 1295–1307 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1343">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151–1165 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1347">11</a></sup>.</p><p>The peanut subgenomes are closely related<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A study of the relationships of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its most closely related wild species using intron sequences and microsatellite markers. Ann. Bot. 111, 113–126 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1354">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nielen, S. et al. Matita, a new retroelement from peanut: characterization and evolutionary context in the light of the Arachis A-B genome divergence. Mol. Genet. Genomics 287, 21–38 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1357">12</a></sup>. This, together with a total genome size of <span class="stix">∼</span>2.7 Gb and an estimated repetitive content of 64% (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dhillon, S.S., Rake, A.V. &amp; Miksche, J.P. Reassociation kinetics and cytophotometric characterization of peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) DNA. Plant Physiol. 65, 1121–1127 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1360">13</a>), makes the assembly of the peanut genome sequence very challenging. However, the A and B subgenomes appear to have undergone relatively few changes since polyploidization: genomic <i>in situ</i> hybridization (GISH), using genomic DNA from the diploid species as probes, clearly distinguishes A and B chromosomes and does not show large A-B mosaics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ramos, M.L. et al. Chromosomal and phylogenetic context for conglutin genes in Arachis based on genomic sequence. Mol. Genet. Genomics 275, 578–592 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1367">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963–1971 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1370">8</a></sup>. Also, the genome size of <i>A. hypogaea</i> is close to the sum of those for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> (1.25 and 1.56 Gb, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Samoluk, S.S. et al. First insight into divergence, representation and chromosome distribution of reverse transcriptase fragments from L1 retrotransposons in peanut and wild relative species. Genetica 143, 113–125 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1384">14</a></sup>), indicating that there has been no large change in genome size since polyploidy. Most notably, observations of progeny derived from crosses between cultivated peanut and an artificially induced allotetraploid <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 × <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 (2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 40)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Fávero, A.P., Simpson, C.E., Valls, F.M.J. &amp; Velo, N.A. Study of evolution of cultivated peanut through crossability studies among Arachis ipaensis, A. duranensis and A. hypogaea. Crop Sci. 46, 1546–1552 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1401">15</a></sup> strongly support the close relationships between the diploid genomes and the corresponding subgenomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i>. Progeny are vigorous, phenotypically normal and fertile and showed lower segregation distortion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Foncéka, D. et al. Genetic mapping of wild introgressions into cultivated peanut: a way toward enlarging the genetic basis of a recent allotetraploid. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 103 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1408">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Integrated consensus map of cultivated peanut and wild relatives reveals structures of the A and B genomes of Arachis and divergence of the legume genomes. DNA Res. 20, 173–184 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1411">17</a></sup> than has been observed for some populations derived from <i>A. hypogaea</i> intraspecific crosses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hong, Y. et al. A SSR-based composite genetic linkage map for the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) genome. BMC Plant Biol. 10, 17 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1418">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gautami, B. et al. An international reference consensus genetic map with 897 marker loci based on 11 mapping populations for tetraploid groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). PLoS One 7, e41213 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1421">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Qin, H. et al. An integrated genetic linkage map of cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) constructed from two RIL populations. Theor. Appl. Genet. 124, 653–664 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1424">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. In silico polymorphism analysis for the development of simple sequence repeat and transposon markers and construction of linkage map in cultivated peanut. BMC Plant Biol. 12, 80 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1427">21</a></sup>. Therefore, as a first step to characterizing the genome of cultivated peanut, we sequenced and analyzed the genomes of the two diploid ancestors of cultivated peanut.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of the diploid A and B genomes</h3><p>Considering that <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 and <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 are likely good representatives of the ancestral species of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, we sequenced their genomes. After filtering, the data generated from the seven paired-end libraries corresponded to an estimated 154× and 163× base-pair coverage for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 1–6</a>). The total assembly sizes were 1,211 and 1,512 Mb for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively, of which 1,081 and 1,371 Mb were represented in scaffolds of 10 kb or greater in size (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 7</a>). Ultradense genetic maps were generated through genotyping by sequencing (GBS) of two diploid recombinant inbred line (RIL) populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM219">Supplementary Data Set 1</a>). SNPs within scaffolds were used to validate the assemblies and confirmed their high quality; 190 of 1,297 initial scaffolds of <i>A. duranensis</i> and 49 of 353 initial scaffolds of <i>A. ipaensis</i> were identified as chimeric, on the basis of the presence of diagnostic population-wide switches in genotype calls occurring at the point of misjoin. Chimeric scaffolds were split, and their components were remapped. Thus, approximate chromosomal placements were obtained for 1,692 and 459 genetically verified scaffolds, respectively. Conventional molecular marker maps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM220">Supplementary Data Set 2</a>) and syntenic inferences were then used to refine the ordering of scaffolds within the initial genetic bins. Generally, agreement was good for maps in euchromatic arms and poorer in pericentromeric regions (although one map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nagy, E.D. et al. A high-density genetic map of Arachis duranensis, a diploid ancestor of cultivated peanut. BMC Genomics 13, 469 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1484">22</a></sup> showed large inversions in two linkage groups in comparison to the other maps; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM220">Supplementary Data Set 2</a>). Overall, 96.0% and 99.2% of the sequence in contigs ≥10,000 bp in length, represented by 1,692 and 459 scaffolds, could be ordered into 10 chromosomal pseudomolecules per genome of 1,025 and 1,338 Mb for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively (Aradu.A01–Aradu.A10 and Araip.B01–Araip.B10; GenBank, assembly accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000817695.1/">GCA_000817695.1</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000816755.1/">GCA_000816755.1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 8</a>). The pseudomolecules mostly showed one-to-one equivalence between the A and B genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig1">Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig2">2</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 1–12</a>) and were numbered according to previously published linkage maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Integrated consensus map of cultivated peanut and wild relatives reveals structures of the A and B genomes of Arachis and divergence of the legume genomes. DNA Res. 20, 173–184 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1525">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gautami, B. et al. An international reference consensus genetic map with 897 marker loci based on 11 mapping populations for tetraploid groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). PLoS One 7, e41213 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1528">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A microsatellite-based, gene-rich linkage map for the AA genome of Arachis (Fabaceae). Theor. Appl. Genet. 111, 1060–1071 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1531">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A linkage map for the B-genome of Arachis (Fabaceae) and its synteny to the A-genome. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 40 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1534">24</a></sup>. They represent 82% and 86% of the genomes, respectively, when considering genome size estimates based on flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Samoluk, S.S. et al. First insight into divergence, representation and chromosome distribution of reverse transcriptase fragments from L1 retrotransposons in peanut and wild relative species. Genetica 143, 113–125 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1538">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Samoluk, S.S., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, J.G. Genome sizes in diploid and allopolyploid Arachis L. species (section Arachis). Genet. Resour. Crop Evol. 62, 747–763 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1541">25</a></sup>, or 95% and 98% of the genomes when using estimates derived from <i>k</i>-mer frequencies with <i>k</i> = 17 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">14</a>). Comparisons of the chromosomal pseudomolecules with 14 BAC sequences from <i>A. duranensis</i> and 6 BAC sequences from <i>A. ipaensis</i> showed collinearity of contigs and high sequence identity (≥99%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 15a–l</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Structural overview and comparison of chromosomal pseudomolecules A01 and B01."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Structural overview and comparison of chromosomal pseudomolecules A01 and B01.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="560"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The distributions of genes and mobile elements are represented as stacked areas. High frequency of genetic recombination (represented by red on a white-red heat scale) is confined to distal regions. In the dot-plot comparison, note how inverted chromosome regions form arcs, indicating that, over the evolutionary time since the divergence of the two species, accumulation of DNA has been greater in more central regions of the chromosomes and elimination of DNA has been more frequent in distal regions. Genes, DNA transposable elements (TEs) and Ty1-<i>copia</i> elements are more frequent in more distal regions. Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> elements are more frequent in pericentromeric regions.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM213">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" duranensis and A. ipaensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Circos diagram depicting the relationships of the chromosomal pseudomolecules of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" represents the density of Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> elements and non-autonomous LTR retrotransposons. The scale for the gray bars is in megabases.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM214">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Characterization of transposons</h3><p>We identified transposable elements that contributed 61.7% and 68.5% of the <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">11</a>; PeanutBase). These values are compatible with the 64% repetitive content estimated for cultivated peanut using renaturation kinetics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Dhillon, S.S., Rake, A.V. &amp; Miksche, J.P. Reassociation kinetics and cytophotometric characterization of peanut (Arachis hypogaea L.) DNA. Plant Physiol. 65, 1121–1127 (1980)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1658">13</a></sup>. Most transposon families were shared by the two genomes, and, for abundant families, macroscale positioning in the two genomes seemed similar. However, because of transposon activity since the divergence of the two genomes, microscale positioning and relative abundance differed (data not shown). A few Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> and non-autonomous retrotransposon families were very abundant, forming dense accumulations in pericentromeres (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">17</a>). These included the previously described autonomous/non-autonomous pairs FIDEL/Feral and Pipoka/Pipa, the non-autonomous Gordo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Nielen, S. et al. FIDEL—a retrovirus-like retrotransposon and its distinct evolutionary histories in the A- and B-genome components of cultivated peanut. Chromosome Res. 18, 227–246 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1675">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bertioli, D.J. et al. The repetitive component of the A genome of peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its role in remodelling intergenic sequence space since its evolutionary divergence from the B genome. Ann. Bot. 112, 545–559 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1678">27</a></sup>, and the newly observed Apolo and Polo. Overall, long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons comprised more than half of each genome. In contrast, DNA transposons constituted about 10%. Notably, 7.8% and 11.7% of the genomes could be attributed to long interspersed nuclear elements (LINEs) for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively. These are the highest coverages for LINEs thus far reported for plant genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene annotation and analysis of gene duplications</h3><p>Transcript assemblies were constructed using sequences expressed in diverse tissues of <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167, <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 and <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Holbrook, C.C. &amp; Culbreath, A.K. Registration of 'Tifrunner' peanut. J. Plant Registr. 1, 124 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1706">28</a></sup> (16,439,433, 21,406,315 and 2,064,268,316 paired-end reads for each species, respectively; details below and in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">13</a>). Using these assemblies and representative characterized transposon sequences, MAKER2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1716">29</a>) delineated 36,734 and 41,840 high-quality non–transposable element genes for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively (PeanutBase). The elevated gene numbers in <i>A. ipaensis</i> appear to originate from more local duplications, which can be seen in counts of genomically 'close' paralogous genes. Considering similar genes within a ten-gene window, there were 25% more in <i>A. ipaensis</i> than in <i>A. duranensis</i> (7,825 versus 6,241). Gene families known to occur in clusters such as those encoding NB-ARC, leucine-rich repeat (LRR), pentatricopeptide-repeat, kinase, WD40-repeat and kinesin proteins had large differential counts between the two genomes. These differences were also apparent with wider inspection. In a set of 9,236 gene families with members in <i>A. ipaensis</i> or <i>A. duranensis</i>, or both, 2,879 families had more members in <i>A. ipaensis</i>, 1,983 had more members in <i>A. duranensis</i> and 4,374 had the same number of members in both species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Data Sets 3–5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">DNA methylation</h3><p>Analysis of DNA methylation by whole-genome bisulfite sequencing using MethylC-seq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lister, R. et al. Highly integrated single-base resolution maps of the epigenome in Arabidopsis. Cell 133, 523–536 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1759">30</a></sup> generated 189,653,337 and 277,101,705 uniquely aligned reads, giving <i><span class="stix">∼</span></i>8.6× and 10.0× coverage per strand for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively. Genome-wide methylation per cytosine content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Schultz, M.D., Schmitz, R.J. &amp; Ecker, J.R. 'Leveling' the playing field for analyses of single-base resolution DNA methylomes. Trends Genet. 28, 583–585 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1772">31</a></sup> was similar for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, with 73% and 75% methylation at CG sites, 57% and 60% methylation at CHG sites (where H is an A, T or C), and 8% and 6% methylation at CHH sites, respectively. The genic methylation patterns were typical for plants and provide independent verification of gene annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Zemach, A., McDaniel, I.E., Silva, P. &amp; Zilberman, D. Genome-wide evolutionary analysis of eukaryotic DNA methylation. Science 328, 916–919 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1783">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Feng, S. et al. Conservation and divergence of methylation patterning in plants and animals. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 8689–8694 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1786">33</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">19</a>; Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO), <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE71357">GSE71357</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Disease resistances and NB-LRR–encoding genes</h3><p>Nucleotide-binding–leucine-rich repeat (NB-LRR)-encoding genes are of particular interest because they confer resistance against pests and diseases. We identified 345 and 397 of these genes in the <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> genotypes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM224">Supplementary Data Set 6</a>). The largest clusters were on distal regions of chromosomal pseudomolecule 02, the lower arms of chromosomal pseudomolecule 04 and the upper arms of chromosomal pseudomolecule 09 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 20</a>). The genome assemblies allowed us to associate quantitative trait loci (QTLs) with candidate genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM225">Supplementary Data Set 7</a>). A strong, consistent QTL for resistance to root-knot nematode (<i>Meloidogyne arenaria</i> (Neal.) Chitwood) identified on A02 of <i>Arachis stenosperma</i> V10309 Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg. (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Leal-Bertioli, S.C.M. et al. Genetic mapping of resistance to Meloidogyne arenaria in Arachis stenosperma: a new source of nematode resistance for peanut. G3 (Bethesda) 6, 377–390 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1833">34</a>) resides in a cluster of 38 NB-LRR–encoding genes covering 6.1 Mb. Another source of nematode resistance already widely used in the United States originates from an introgression of the A-genome species <i>Arachis cardenasii</i> Krapov. &amp; W.C. Greg. (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Burow, M.D., Simpson, C.E., Paterson, A.H. &amp; Starr, J.L. Identification of peanut (Arachis hypogaea) RAPD markers diagnostic of root-knot nematode (Meloidogyne arenaria (Neal) Chitwood) resistance. Mol. Breed. 2, 369–379 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1839">35</a>). This segment resides in the upper distal 7.6 Mb of chromosome A09 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Nagy, E. et al. Recombination is suppressed in an alien introgression in peanut harboring Rma, a dominant root-knot nematode resistance gene. Mol. Breed. 26, 357–370 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1842">36</a>) and contains many NB-LRR–encoding genes. A major QTL conferring reduction in lesion number, size and sporulation of rust was identified in <i>Arachis magna</i> K30097 Krapov., W.C. Greg. &amp; C.E. Simpson<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Leal-Bertioli, S.C. et al. Identification of QTLs for rust resistance in the peanut wild species Arachis magna and the development of KASP markers for marker assisted selection. G3 (Bethesda) 5, 1403–1413 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1850">37</a></sup>. The closest linked marker (Ah280; Araip.B08, 126,645,511) maps close to an NB-LRR–encoding gene (Araip.RV63R). Another QTL for rust resistance has previously been identified in peanut varieties that have the wild A-genome species <i>A. cardenasii</i> in their pedigree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sujay, V. et al. Quantitative trait locus analysis and construction of consensus genetic map for foliar disease resistance based on two recombinant inbred line populations in cultivated groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). Mol. Breed. 30, 773–788 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d23641e1857">38</a></sup>. Markers mapped this QTL to an introgressed chromosome segment at the lower end of A03 (Aradu.A03, 131,305,113–133,690,542) where an NB-LRR–encoding gene resides in <i>A. duranensis</i> (Aradu.Z87JB). The genes harbored on these genome segments from <i>A. stenosperma</i>, <i>A. magna</i> and <i>A. cardenasii</i> provide good pest and disease resistance and warrant further investigation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene evolution in <i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i> and species divergence</h3><p>Analyses suggest that the <i>Arachis</i> lineages have been accumulating mutations relatively quickly since the divergence of the Dalbergioid clade <span class="stix">∼</span>58 million years ago. Modal <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> paralog values (synonymous substitutions per synonymous site) are approximately 0.95 for <i>A. ipaensis</i> and 0.90 for <i>A. duranensis</i>, more similar to that for <i>Medicago</i> (paralogous <i>K</i>s value of <span class="stix">∼</span>0.95) than to those of <i>Lotus</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.65), <i>Glycine</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.65) or <i>Phaseolus</i> (<span class="stix">∼</span>0.80). Average rates of change for <i>Arachis</i> genes were estimated at 8.12 × 10<sup>−9</sup> <i>K</i><sub>S</sub>/year. On the basis of this and the peak in the frequency of <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values between <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> being 0.035, the divergence of the two species was estimated as occurring <span class="stix">∼</span>2.16 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig3">Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">22</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Mutations and genome duplications."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Mutations and genome duplications.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="530"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Frequency distributions are shown of values of synonymous substitutions (<i>K</i><sub>S</sub>) for paralogous and orthologous genes in comparisons of <i>A. duranensis</i> (Ad), <i>A. ipaensis</i> (Ai) and <i>Glycine max</i> (Gm). Peaks in the <i>G. max</i>–<i>G. max</i> comparison represent the <i>Glycine</i> whole-genome duplication (WGD) at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> = 0.10 (<span class="stix">∼</span>10 million years ago) and the early papilionoid WGD at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> = 0.65 (58 million years ago). The same early papilionoid WGD also affected the <i>Arachis</i> lineage, so the shift in the <i>A. duranensis</i>–<i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>–<i>A. ipaensis</i> peaks (at <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> = 0.90 and 0.95, respectively) indicates that <i>Arachis</i> has accumulated silent changes at a rate <span class="stix">∼</span>1.4 times faster than that in <i>G. max</i>. On the basis of average rates of change for <i>Arachis</i> of 8.12 × 10<sup>−9</sup> <i>K</i><sub>S</sub>/year, we estimate that <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> diverged <span class="stix">∼</span>2.16 million years ago.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM215">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Analysis of chromosomal structure and synteny</h3><p>In accordance with cytogenetic observations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relationships among the wild B genome of Arachis species (section Arachis) based on FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection: a new proposal for genome arrangement. Theor. Appl. Genet. 121, 1033–1046 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2056">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Robledo, G., Lavia, G.I. &amp; Seijo, G. Species relations among wild Arachis species with the A genome as revealed by FISH mapping of rDNA loci and heterochromatin detection. Theor. Appl. Genet. 118, 1295–1307 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2059">10</a></sup>, most pseudomolecules had symmetrically positioned pericentromeres. Most pseudomolecules showed a one-to-one correspondence between the two species: pairs 02, 03, 04 and 10 were collinear; pairs 05, 06 and 09 were each differentiated by a large inversion in one arm of one of the pseudomolecules; and the pseudomolecules in pair 01 were differentiated by large inversions of both arms (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig1">Figs. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig2">2</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 1–12</a>). In contrast, chromosomes 07 and 08 have undergone complex rearrangements that transported repeat-rich DNA to A07 and gene-rich DNA to A08. As a result, A07 has only one normal (upper) euchromatic arm and A08 is abnormally small, with low repetitive content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig4">Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 11</a>). In accordance with cytogenetic observations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Seijo, G. et al. Genomic relationships between the cultivated peanut (Arachis hypogaea, Leguminosae) and its close relatives revealed by double GISH. Am. J. Bot. 94, 1963–1971 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2079">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Nielen, S. et al. FIDEL—a retrovirus-like retrotransposon and its distinct evolutionary histories in the A- and B-genome components of cultivated peanut. Chromosome Res. 18, 227–246 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2082">26</a></sup>, A08 could be assigned as the characteristic small 'A chromosome' (cytogenetic chromosome A09; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Schematic showing the rearrangements between chromosomes 7 and 8."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Schematic showing the rearrangements between chromosomes 7 and 8.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1059"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>These rearrangements gave rise to the small, repeat-poor chromosome, represented by pseudomolecule Aradu.A08 (equivalent to cytogenetic A09), which is characteristic of A genomes, and Aradu.A07, which has only one normal euchromatic arm (the upper one). Syntenous chromosomal segments are represented by blocks of the same color. The Ty3-<i>gypsy</i> and non-autonomous retroelement distributions are represented in gray. Note the low repetitive content of Aradu.A08 and the 'knob' of repeat-rich DNA in the upper distal region. This unusual composition seems likely to account for the distinct chromatin condensation of this chromosome pair (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>All <i>A. ipaensis</i> pseudomolecules were larger than their <i>A. duranensis</i> counterparts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 8</a>). This is partly because of a greater frequency of local duplications and higher transposon content in <i>A. ipaensis</i>. In dot plots of collinear chromosomes, slopes formed by orthologous genes were similar in both euchromatic and pericentromeric regions, with <i>A. duranensis</i> regions being <span class="stix">∼</span>80–90% the length of the corresponding regions in <i>A. ipaensis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 2–4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">12</a>). In contrast, in the dot plots, chromosomal regions differentiated by inversions showed distinct arcs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 1, 5, 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">11</a>). These arcs are due to changes in rates of DNA loss and gain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bennetzen, J.L., Ma, J. &amp; Devos, K.M. Mechanisms of recent genome size variation in flowering plants. Ann. Bot. 95, 127–132 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2153">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Tian, Z. et al. Do genetic recombination and gene density shape the pattern of DNA elimination in rice long terminal repeat retrotransposons? Genome Res. 19, 2221–2230 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2156">40</a></sup> in regions that switch from distal to pericentromeric contexts, or vice versa, when inverted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig5">Fig. 5</a>). In chromosomes without inversions, there were characteristic density gradients for genes, repetitive DNA and methylation (with gene densities increasing and densities of repetitive DNA and methylation decreasing toward chromosome ends). However, in regions that had undergone large rearrangements, in <i>A. duranensis</i>, these gradients were disrupted (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 16, 17</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">24–27</a>). From these observations, we concluded that the major rearrangements have all occurred in the A-genome lineage. Size differences between homeologous chromosomes that were differentiated by large rearrangements tended to be greater than those between collinear ones (<i>r</i>(6) = 0.65, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 14</a>). Because the <i>A. duranensis</i> chromosomes that have undergone inversions are smaller than expected, it is evident that, in this dynamic, on balance, the elimination of DNA has predominated over its accumulation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Model for the formation of the arcs in dot plots of genome regions that have been inverted since the divergence of the A and B genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Model for the formation of the arcs in dot plots of genome regions that have been inverted since the divergence of the A and B genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="626"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Gene densities are shown in gray. (<b>a</b>) The inversion transports repeat-rich, gene-poor DNA to the distal chromosomal region and repeat-poor, gene-rich DNA to the more central region. (<b>b</b>) In the distal region, the inverted segment then loses DNA by recombination-driven deletion, and the more central region gains DNA. (<b>c</b>) Thus, the characteristic arc and atypical gene, repetitive DNA and methylation density patterns are formed. The presence of these atypical patterns indicates that all major genome rearrangements occurred in the A-genome lineage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 16, 24</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">26</a>). (<b>d</b>) An example dot plot comparing A05 and B05 that shows the characteristic arc.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Comparisons with <i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i> L., which shared a common ancestor with <i>Arachis</i> about 58 million years ago, showed syntenous chromosomal segments. In some cases, although the dot plots were highly distorted, there was almost a one-to-one correspondence between chromosomes (for example, B01 and Pv03, B05 and Pv02, B06 and Pv01, and B08 and Pv05; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Figs. 28–31</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Sequence comparisons to tetraploid cultivated peanut</h3><p>Comparisons showed fundamentally one-to-one correspondences between the diploid chromosomal pseudomolecules and cultivated peanut linkage groups. Of the marker sequences from three maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. In silico polymorphism analysis for the development of simple sequence repeat and transposon markers and construction of linkage map in cultivated peanut. BMC Plant Biol. 12, 80 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2244">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genomics 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2247">41</a></sup>, 83%, 83% and 94% were assigned by sequence similarity searches to the expected diploid chromosomal pseudomolecules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 15a–c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM226">Supplementary Data Set 8</a>). For more detailed genome-wide comparisons, we produced 5.74 Gb (2× coverage) of long-sequence Moleculo reads from <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner and mapped the reads to the combined diploid pseudomolecules. The corrected median identities between the <i>A. hypogaea</i> Moleculo reads and the pseudomolecules of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> were 98.36% and 99.96%, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM224">Supplementary Data Set 6</a>). When visualized as plots along the chromosomal pseudomolecules, the diploid A-genome chromosomes were distinctly less similar to <i>A. hypogaea</i> sequences than the B-genome chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig6">Fig. 6</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 32a–t</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM227">Supplementary Data Set 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Example graphs comparing DNA sequences from cultivated peanut with chromosomal pseudomolecules of A. duranensis and A. ipaensis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Example graphs comparing DNA sequences from cultivated peanut with chromosomal pseudomolecules of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="558"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>,<b>b</b>) Graphs show mapping of Moleculo DNA sequence reads from the tetraploid <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner along the diploid chromosomal pseudomolecules Aradu.A05 (<b>a</b>) and Araip.B05 (<b>b</b>). Dark blue dots represent percentage identity of reads in tiling paths, and red dots represent density of Moleculo bases mapping in windows of 0.5 Mb (normalized to a value of 1 for the expected number). Note how the percentage identities of mapped reads for Aradu.A05 are, contrary to expectation, more consistent in the pericentromeric regions than in distal ones. This may reflect that sequence similarity between <i>A. duranensis</i> and the A subgenome of <i>A. hypogaea</i> has been eroded by recombination between the A and B subgenomes in the tetraploid by, for example, gene conversion. In contrast, mapping on Araip.B05 is much more consistent and generally very high identity, except for the upper distal 6.1 Mb, where identities fall dramatically (blue arrow). Also note how deviations in expected mapping density (indicated by red arrows) show that this region, in the tetraploid genome of <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner, has undergone tetrasomic recombination and has changed from the expected genome formula of AABB to AAAA.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM216">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We found distinct signals of genetic recombination between the A and B subgenomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, and, as expected, these signals were more frequent in regions of the homeologous chromosome pairs that were collinear. This recombination erodes the similarities between the tetraploid subgenomes and their corresponding diploid genomes. We observed a significant tendency for <i>A. hypogaea</i> Moleculo reads that mapped to collinear A-genome pseudomolecules to have, on average, lower sequence identity than reads that mapped to pseudomolecules with inversions (Kruskal-Wallis test, <i>P</i> &lt; 0.0001; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">17</a>, and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM227">Supplementary Data Set 9</a>). This trend was much weaker for the B subgenome on a whole-chromosome scale but was clearly visible at the ends of some of the collinear B-subgenome chromosome arms, where percentage identities dropped dramatically. An example of this is indicated by the blue arrow in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig6">Figure 6b</a>. In this case, the <i>A. hypogaea</i> B subgenome has become nullisomic and the A subgenome has become tetrasomic (producing a genome composition of AAAA instead of the expected AABB). This was confirmed by an inverse symmetry in the total number of bases mapping to the A and B genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig6">Fig. 6a,b, red arrows</a>). This phenomenon, a degree of tetrasomic genetic behavior, has been observed in the progeny of interploidy crosses involving wild species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Garcia, G.M., Stalker, H.T., Shroeder, E. &amp; Kochert, G. Identification of RAPD, SCAR, and RFLP markers tightly linked to nematode resistance genes introgressed from Arachis cardenasii into Arachis hypogaea. Genome 39, 836–845 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2375">42</a></sup> and recently in the cultivated × induced allotetraploid RILs used in this study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Leal-Bertioli, S. et al. Tetrasomic recombination is surprisingly frequent in allotetraploid Arachis. Genetics 199, 1093–1105 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2379">43</a></sup>, but this is the first time, to our knowledge, that it has been observed in pure cultivated peanut. The event depicted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig6">Figure 6</a> affects approximately the top 6 Mb, about half the euchromatic arms of <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner chromosomes 05. Smaller similar events covered the bottom <span class="stix">∼</span>1 Mb of A02 and B02, the bottom <span class="stix">∼</span>0.4 Mb of A03 and B03, the bottom <span class="stix">∼</span>2 Mb of A06 and B06, and the top <span class="stix">∼</span>0.5 Mb of A09 and B09. Because of lower sequence identities, A-subgenome nullisomes were more difficult to detect; nevertheless, the bottom <span class="stix">∼</span>3 Mb of chromosomes 04 appeared to be nullisomic for the A subgenome and tetrasomic for the B subgenome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM227">Supplementary Data Set 9</a>).</p><p>Although we recognize that genetic exchange between the A and B subgenomes will tend to inflate the values calculated (especially for the A genomes), we estimated the dates of evolutionary divergence of the sequenced diploid genomes and the corresponding subgenomes of <i>A. hypogaea</i>. To estimate the genome-wide <i>Arachis</i> mutation rate, we mapped <i>A. ipaensis</i> Moleculo reads against the <i>A. duranensis</i> pseudomolecules. This gave a corrected median DNA identity of 93.11% (a value compatible with previous comparisons using BAC sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Bertioli, D.J. et al. The repetitive component of the A genome of peanut (Arachis hypogaea) and its role in remodelling intergenic sequence space since its evolutionary divergence from the B genome. Ann. Bot. 112, 545–559 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2411">27</a></sup>). Considering the date of divergence of the A and B genomes as 2.16 million years ago (from <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values), this gives an <i>Arachis</i> genome-wide mutation rate of 1.6 × 10<sup>−8</sup> mutations per base per year (within the range of 1–2 × 10<sup>−8</sup> calculated for other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Genome-wide genetic changes during modern breeding of maize. Nat. Genet. 44, 812–815 (2012); corrigendum 46, 1039–1040 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2427">44</a></sup>). This mutation rate and the divergence of the most conserved chromosomes (presumably the ones that have undergone the least recombination between subgenomes; A01 and B07) gives an estimate of the divergence time of <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 from the A subgenome of <i>A. hypogaea</i> as <span class="stix">∼</span>247,000 years and for the divergence time of <i>A. ipaensis</i> from the B subgenome of <i>A. hypogaea</i> as a remarkably recent <span class="stix">∼</span>9,400 years.</p><p>We used the chromosomal pseudomolecules to investigate the frequency of recombination between A and B subgenomes in 166 cultivated peanut RILs described in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genomics 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2447">41</a></sup>. To do this, we calculated the mapping densities of restriction site–associated sequence reads from these RILs and their parental lines along the chromosomal pseudomolecules. Mostly, the relative dosage of mapping on the A and B genomes was equal and the same as in the parents, but for one line (RIL028) the relative dosage was dramatically altered for two homeologous chromosomal regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig7">Fig. 7</a>): 104–112 Mb on Aradu.A04 and 112–126 Mb on Araip.B04. In these regions, mapping to Araip.B04 almost disappeared and mapping to Aradu.A04 dramatically increased in density. This clear signal indicates that genetic exchange occurred between the A and B subgenomes in regions of the cultivated peanut genome that had balanced dosage in the parental lines. This seems most likely to have occurred by tetrasomic recombination, but gene conversion after the formation of an unresolved Holiday junction is also possible.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-7" data-title="Identification of genetic exchange between subgenomes in cultivated peanut."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig7" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 7: Identification of genetic exchange between subgenomes in cultivated peanut.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/7" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig7_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-7-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig7_HTML.jpg" alt="figure7" loading="lazy" width="685" height="634"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-7-desc"><p>The top graph depicts the result of recombination between A04 and B04 in RIL028, and, for comparison, the bottom graph shows RIL025, a typical line where this type of recombination has not occurred (lines described in Zhou et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genomics 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2466">41</a></sup>). The <i>y</i> axis shows log<sub>2</sub>-transformed ratios of densities of mapping for restriction site–associated sequence reads along the diploid chromosomal pseudomolecules divided by the mapping densities of a parental line. The <i>x</i> axis shows the positions of mapping, in 1-Mb windows, along Araip.B04 (red dots) and Aradu.A04 (blue dots), the latter with distances scaled so the homeologous chromosomal pseudomolecules are directly comparable. In RIL028, the relative dosage of the subgenomes has changed greatly in the lower chromosome arms. This indicates that a new event of genetic exchange between the A and B subgenomes occurred.</p><p>
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM217">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/7" data-track-dest="link:Figure7 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Diploid genome–guided tetraploid transcriptome assembly</h3><p>Assemblies of transcribed sequences from tetraploid cultivated peanut are challenging because reads from genes on the A and B subgenomes are erroneously assembled together, resulting in chimeric sequences. We used the diploid genomes to minimize this collapse and produced tetraploid transcript assemblies.
