We assessed four assembly software approaches in three different ways: <i>de novo</i> assembly; parsing into A- and B-genome sets followed by separate assembly; and parsing followed by genome-guided assembly using the combined pseudomolecules. Results were compared by measuring the percentage of assembled transcripts that mapped back to the pseudomolecules without mismatches. Higher percentages indicate less collapse, as collapsed transcripts can only map with mismatches. This analysis showed that the <i>de novo</i> assemblies were the least accurate, with the percentage of mismatch-free mapping ranging from 32.17 to 39.82%, followed by the parsed set assemblies (40.07 to 55.8%). Finally, the genome-guided assembly was the most accurate with 65.87% mismatch-free mapping (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Fig. 33</a>). Using this workflow, together with filtering for transposable elements, low-expression transcripts and redundancy, we obtained 183,062 assembled <i>A. hypogaea</i> transcripts, of which we could tentatively assign 88,643 (48.42%) to the A subgenome and 94,419 (51.58%) to the B subgenome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><p>The peoples of South America have cultivated <i>Arachis</i> species since prehistoric times, but only the allotetraploid <i>A. hypogaea</i> was completely transformed by domestication to become a crop of global importance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W.C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16 (Supl.), 1–205 (2007) [transl.]." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2527">45</a></sup>. As a foundation to investigate peanut's genome, we sequenced its diploid progenitors <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>. Comparisons of the chromosomal pseudomolecules of the diploid species with <i>A. hypogaea</i> show high levels of similarity, but, most notably, the cultivated peanut B subgenome is nearly identical to the genome of <i>A. ipaensis</i>. This similarity suggests a remarkable story for this particular <i>Arachis</i> population dating back to the time of the earliest human occupation of South America.</p><p><i>Arachis</i> species have an unusual reproductive biology; although the flowers develop above ground, a special 'peg' structure (gynophore) pushes the young pod underground, where development is completed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Smith, B. Arachis hypogaea, aerial flower and subterranean fruit. Am. J. Bot. 37, 802–850 (1950)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2552">46</a></sup>. The seeds are protected and have privileged access to water at the beginning of the rainy season. However, they are not usually dispersed and germinate within an area of roughly 1 m in diameter covered by the mother plant. Therefore, populations are quite static over long periods of time: over a thousand years, they can usually move only about 1 km. Rarely, water-driven soil erosion will disperse seeds downhill. This pattern of dispersal, coupled with a high rate of self-pollination, has led to species distributions that consist of patchy, often highly homozygous populations distributed over areas defined by major river systems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bertioli, D.J. et al. An overview of peanut and its wild relatives. Plant Genet. Resour.; Characterization Util. 9, 134–149 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2556">47</a></sup>.</p><p>Both sequenced accessions were collected in the most likely geographic region for the origin of cultivated peanut (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#Fig8">Fig. 8</a>). Whereas <i>A. duranensis</i> is represented by numerous, genetically diverse populations in the region, <i>A. ipaensis</i> is only known to be from a single location (from where it may now have disappeared; G. Seijo, personal communication). This site of occurrence of <i>A. ipaensis</i> is incompatible with natural seed dispersion: the closest relative of <i>A. ipaensis</i>, the biologically conspecific <i>A. magna</i>, occurs in numerous natural populations more than 500 km to the north and more than 200 m lower in altitude<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W.C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16 (Supl.), 1–205 (2007) [transl.]." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2582">45</a></sup>. Therefore, it seems most likely that humans transported the seed that founded this population, and several lines of evidence indicate that this same population was involved in the formation of <i>A. hypogaea</i>. <i>A. ipaensis</i> is the only B-genome <i>Arachis</i> species ever found within the range of <i>A. duranensis</i>. Its site of occurrence is immediately adjacent to the center of diversity for the most primitive of the botanical varieties of cultivated peanut (<i>A. hypogaea</i> subsp. <i>hypogaea</i> var. <i>hypogaea</i>), and, most notably, it has extremely high DNA similarity with the B subgenome of <i>A. hypogaea</i>. We estimate that they diverged <span class="stix">∼</span>9,400 years ago, at a time when the region was becoming populated by early inhabitants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Dillehay, T.D. et al. Monte Verde: seaweed, food, medicine, and the peopling of South America. Science 320, 784–786 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2611">48</a></sup>. The earliest archeological records of <i>Arachis</i> cultivation are about 7,800 years old from Peru, far from the most likely region of origin for <i>A. hypogaea</i> or any natural <i>Arachis</i> species distribution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Dillehay, T.D., Rossen, J., Andres, T.C. &amp; Williams, D.E. Preceramic adoption of peanut, squash, and cotton in northern Peru. Science 316, 1890–1893 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2625">49</a></sup>. It seems likely that <i>Arachis</i> cultivation would have started within the native range well before then<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Grabiele, M., Chalup, L., Robledo, G. &amp; Seijo, G. Genetic and geographic origin of domesticated peanut as evidenced by 5S rDNA and chloroplast DNA sequences. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1151–1165 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2632">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Simpson, C.E., Krapovickas, A. &amp; Valls, J.F.M. History of Arachis including evidence of A. hypogaea L. progenitors. Peanut Sci. 28, 78–80 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2635">50</a></sup>. The date of polyploidization is uncertain, but the earliest identifiable remains of <i>A. hypogaea</i> date from <span class="stix">∼</span>3,500–4,500 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Bonavia, D. Precerámico Peruano. Los Gavilanes. Mar, Desierto y Oasis en La Historia del Hombre (Corporación Financiera de Desarrollo and Instituto Arqueológico Alemán, 1982)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2643">51</a></sup>. For most plants, following polyploidization, sequence identity between the diploid progenitors and the polyploid subgenomes would have been dispersed by genetic recombination in subsequent generations. However, in the case of the B genomes, it persisted, perhaps owing to extreme genetic bottlenecks and reproductive isolation in both species (<i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. hypogaea</i>). We think that this is a unique find among crop plants, which was possible because of the peculiar biology of the genus and the remarkable work of botanical collectors.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-8" data-title="The approximate known distributions of A. duranensis and A. magna, the location of the single known occurrence of A. ipaensis and the center of diversity for the most primitive type of cultivated peanut, A. hypogaea subsp. hypogaea var. hypogaea."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig8" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 8: The approximate known distributions of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. magna</i>, the location of the single known occurrence of <i>A. ipaensis</i> and the center of diversity for the most primitive type of cultivated peanut, <i>A. hypogaea</i> subsp. <i>hypogaea</i> var. <i>hypogaea</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/8" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig8_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-8-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3517/MediaObjects/41588_2016_Article_BFng3517_Fig8_HTML.jpg" alt="figure8" loading="lazy" width="685" height="639"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-8-desc"><p><i>A. ipaensis</i> is only known to be from a single location and is biologically conspecific with <i>A. magna</i>, which occurs far to the north and at lower altitude. The isolated occurrence and estimated divergence of the <i>A. ipaensis</i> genome from the B genome of <i>A. hypogaea</i>, only <span class="stix">∼</span>9,400 years ago, indicate that <i>A. ipaensis</i> was probably taken to its present location from the north by prehistoric inhabitants of the region. <i>A. hypogaea</i> was formed by hybridization of <i>A. ipaensis</i> with <i>A. duranensis</i> and polyploidization. The figure was generated using Natural Earth.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3517/figures/8" data-track-dest="link:Figure8 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Building on their tractability as genetic systems, we sequenced peanut's diploid ancestors. We used them to identify candidate pest and disease resistance genes, to reduce collapse in tetraploid transcriptome assemblies and to show the impact of recombination between subgenomes in cultivated peanut. The availability of these genomes will lead to further advances in knowledge of genetic changes since the very recent polyploidization event that gave rise to cultivated peanut and to the production of better tools for molecular breeding and crop improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Species accessions for genome sequencing.</h3><p>Stock seeds were from the Brazilian <i>Arachis</i> germplasm collection, maintained at Embrapa Genetic Resources and Biotechnology (Brasília, Brazil). Plants were maintained in pollinator-proof greenhouses. To sequence the A genome, <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 was used: this yellow-flowered accession was collected by J.F.M. Valls, L. Novara and A. Etcheverryin in 1997 from Ruta Nacional 51, Estación Alvarado, near Tabacalera, Argentina, 24° 50′ 18.4′′ S, 65° 27′ 28.9′′ W, at an elevation of 1,206 m. To sequence the B genome, <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 was used. This accession, collected by A. Krapovickas, W.C. Gregory, D.J. Banks, J.R. Pietrarelli, A. Schinini and C.E. Simpson in 1977, is the only available accession in germplasm collections worldwide. It originated from the same collection site as the holotype of this species, the species' only known site of occurrence, <i><span class="stix">∼</span></i>30 km north of Villa Montes, Bolivia. The site was originally recorded with the techniques available in 1977 as 21° S, 63° 25′ W, at an elevation of 650 m (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Krapovickas, A. &amp; Gregory, W.C. Taxonomy of the genus Arachis (Leguminosae). Bonplandia 16 (Supl.), 1–205 (2007) [transl.]." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2743">45</a>) but, considering the topology of the region and the description of the site of occurrence, was more probably near Camatindi about 21° 00′ 01′′ S, 63° 23′ 37′′ W, at an elevation of <span class="stix">∼</span>600 m, or in or near Tigüipa (J.F.M. Valls and C. Simpson, personal communication).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing and assembly.</h3><p><i>Sequence generation.</i> Illumina HiSeq 2000 paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes of 250 bp, 500 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb were constructed following the manufacturer's instructions. Libraries with 40-kb inserts were constructed using a fosmid-based method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zhang, G. et al. Genome sequence of foxtail millet (Setaria italica) provides insights into grass evolution and biofuel potential. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 549–554 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2757">52</a></sup>. In total, 14 and 19 sequencing libraries were constructed for <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, respectively, from which we generated 325.73 Gb of raw data reads for <i>A. duranensis</i> and 416.59 Gb of raw data reads for <i>A. ipaensis</i>, with read lengths from 90–150 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 1–5</a>).</p><p><i>Quality filtering.</i> The following lower-quality reads were discarded: reads with more than 5% Ns or with polyadenylated termini; reads from the short-insert libraries (170–800 bp) with 20 or more bases having quality scores ≤7; reads from the large-insert libraries (2–40 kb) with 40 or more bases having quality score ≤7; reads with adaptor contamination (more than 10 bp aligned to the adaptor sequence when allowing ≤3 bp of mismatches); reads with read 1 and read 2 having ≥10 bp overlapping (allowing 10% mismatches; except for the 250-bp insert library, where the paired reads should overlap); reads identical to each other at both ends that might have been caused by PCR duplication; and reads where the quality of the bases at the head or tail was ≤7.</p><p><i>k-mer analysis. k</i>-mers were extracted from sequences generated from the short-insert libraries, and the frequencies were calculated and plotted. Genome sizes were estimated by dividing the total numbers of <i>k-</i>mers by the depths of the major peaks.</p><p><i>Error correction.</i> We also used <i>k</i>-mers to correct for errors. For sequencing with high depth, the <i>k</i>-mers without any sequencing errors should appear multiple times in the read data set, whereas error-containing <i>k</i>-mers should have low frequencies. We corrected sequencing errors in the 17-mers with frequencies lower than 3 in the clean data for the 250-bp and 500-bp insert libraries</p><p><i>Genome assembly.</i> COPE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Liu, B. et al. COPE: an accurate k-mer–based pair-end reads connection tool to facilitate genome assembly. Bioinformatics 28, 2870–2874 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2810">53</a></sup> was used to join paired-end reads from the 250-bp insert library into single longer reads of <span class="stix">∼</span>250 bp. Genome assembly was performed using SOAPdenovo version 2.05 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265–272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2813">54</a>), with parameters --K 81 --R. Gaps were filled using KGF and Gapcloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2817">55</a></sup> (version 1.10). Finally, SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C.V., Jansen, H.J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2821">56</a></sup> was used to further link the scaffolds where connections were supported by more than five paired reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Production of Moleculo synthetic long reads.</h3><p>TruSeq synthetic long-read sequencing libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="McCoy, R.C. et al. Illumina TruSeq synthetic long-reads empower de novo assembly and resolve complex, highly-repetitive transposable elements. PLoS One 9, e106689 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2833">57</a></sup> were generated by Moleculo and Illumina as part of beta tests of this technology. Fifteen libraries were generated for <i>A. duranensis</i> K7988, and each was sequenced on a HiSeq 2500 lane; the PE100 reads were assembled into 1.5 million TruSeq (Moleculo) synthetic long reads, providing approximately 5× genome coverage with a mean read length of 3,684 bases and an N50 of 4,344 bases. Twelve libraries were used for <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 to yield approximately 2 million Moleculo reads with mean length of 4,054 bases and an N50 length of 5,152 bases, providing <span class="stix">∼</span>6× genome coverage. Thirteen libraries were used for <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner, which produced 1,263,111 Moleculo reads with a mean length of 4,547 bases and an N50 length of 6,137 bases, providing 2.3× genome coverage. These reads were used for genome comparisons and were not incorporated in the diploid genome assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Mapping populations.</h3><p>Three RIL mapping populations derived by single-seed descent were used, a diploid A-genome population composed of 90 F<sub>5</sub> individuals derived from <i>A. duranensis</i> K7988 and <i>A. stenosperma</i> V10309, a diploid B-genome RIL population composed of 94 F<sub>6</sub> individuals derived from a cross between <i>A. ipaensis</i> KG30076 and <i>A. magna</i> KG30097, and a tetraploid AB RIL population composed of 89 F<sub>6</sub> individuals derived from a cross between <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Runner IAC 886 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Godoy, I.J. et al. Cultivares de Amendoim: Novas Opções para o Mercado de Confeitaria (Campinas: Instituto Agronômico, 2003)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2875">58</a>) and a colchicine-induced tetraploid <i>A. ipaensis</i> K30076 × <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 (2<i>n</i> = 4<i>x</i> = 40)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Fávero, A.P., Simpson, C.E., Valls, F.M.J. &amp; Velo, N.A. Study of evolution of cultivated peanut through crossability studies among Arachis ipaensis, A. duranensis and A. hypogaea. Crop Sci. 46, 1546–1552 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2892">15</a></sup>. Populations were developed and maintained in pollinator-proof greenhouses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Linkage maps and identification of misjoins.</h3><p><i>Conventional molecular marker maps from diploid A and B genomes and cultivated peanut × induced allotetraploid RIL populations.</i> Linkage maps were constructed using Mapmaker Macintosh 2.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lander, E.S. et al. MAPMAKER: an interactive computer package for constructing primary genetic linkage maps of experimental and natural populations. Genomics 1, 174–181 (1987)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2906">59</a>) or as previously published. For details, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p><i>Genetic maps generated from genotyping-by-sequencing data for diploid A- and B-genome RIL populations and identification of chimeric scaffolds</i>. Recombinant inbred lines from the diploid A- and B-genome populations were shotgun sequenced to 1× genome coverage with paired-end 100-bp reads on a HiSeq 2500 sequencer. The parents were sequenced at 20× genome coverage. Parental-homozygous SNPs were identified by alignments to the scaffolds of the <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> genome assemblies as well as local realignment and probabilistic variant calling in CLC Genomics Workbench (CLC Bio). Filtering in CLC Workbench resulted in about 3 million high-quality homozygous-parental SNPs for both A- and B-genome mapping population parents. The coordinates of these SNPs were converted into BED format, and the alignment data at the SNP coordinates were extracted with SAMtools mpileup<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2924">60</a></sup>. From the low-coverage sequencing data, groups of 20 consecutive SNPs were haplotyped with a set of custom Python scripts. Genotype calls were inspected visually and by a hidden Markov model (HMM) script (courtesy of I. Korf, University of California, Davis) to identify population-wide switches in genotype calls corresponding to scaffold misjoins. Scaffolds not displaying recombination for an individual RIL were haplotyped. Linkage groups were identified from the haplotyping data using MadMapper and Carthagene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Schiex, T. &amp; Gaspin, C. CARTHAGENE: constructing and joining maximum likelihood genetic maps. Proc. Int. Conf. Intell. Syst. Mol. Biol. 5, 258–267 (1997)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2928">61</a></sup>, applying logarithm of odds (LOD) score thresholds of 8 and distance thresholds of 50 cM; genetic maps were generated with Carthagene using the lkh traveling salesman algorithm and flips, polish and annealing optimizations. Additional scaffolds (indicated in the data files) were added to genetic bins in two rounds of binning with a custom Python script. Misjoined scaffolds were split at breakpoint locations identified by flanking GBS SNP locations, at the 'upstream SNP' and the 'downstream SNP', delineating the switches in genotype calls, and intervening sequence was excluded from the pseudomolecule assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Generation of chromosomal pseudomolecules.</h3><p>Scaffolds less than 10 kb in length were removed (they are available in the full assembly scaffold files at PeanutBase: Adur1.split6.fa and Aipa2s.split7.fa). Sequences were subjected to RepeatMasker using <i>Arachis</i> repeat libraries available at PeanutBase (mobile-elements-AA051914.fasta and mobile-elements-BB051914.fasta). Pseudomolecules were given initial chromosomal placements and orderings according to the GBS maps. Placement was arbitrary within blocks with the same centiMorgan value. Scaffold orientation and placement were refined according to the conventional maps using, in order of priority, the tetraploid AB-genome map, the diploid F<sub>2</sub> A-genome Nagy map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nagy, E.D. et al. A high-density genetic map of Arachis duranensis, a diploid ancestor of cultivated peanut. BMC Genomics 13, 469 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2945">22</a></sup> (for the <i>A. duranensis</i> assembly), the diploid B-genome map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A linkage map for the B-genome of Arachis (Fabaceae) and its synteny to the A-genome. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 40 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2952">24</a></sup> (for the <i>A. ipaensis</i> assembly) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM220">Supplementary Data Set 2</a>) and finally the tetraploid AB-genome Shirasawa map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Integrated consensus map of cultivated peanut and wild relatives reveals structures of the A and B genomes of Arachis and divergence of the legume genomes. DNA Res. 20, 173–184 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23641e2963">17</a></sup>. Markers were located on the scaffolds using BLAST and ePCR (electronic PCR) with high similarity parameters (taking the top hits only, with placement by BLAST (<i>e</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>) given preference over ePCR where both were available). Markers placing scaffolds on linkage groups other than the one assigned by the GBS data were dropped.</p><p>Where allowed by map data, scaffold positions and orientations were adjusted using synteny between the two <i>Arachis</i> species and, where necessary (generally within pericentromeric regions), synteny with <i>G. max</i> and <i>Proteus vulgaris</i>; the presence of telomeric repeats near chromosome ends; information from repeat-masked paired-end sequences from 42,000 BAC clones of <i>A. duranensis</i> V14167 (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss/FI321525">FI321525</a>–<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nucgss/FI281689">FI281689</a>) and Moleculo sequence reads from <i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i>. Apparent inversions were visually inspected and confirmed. Scaffolds with either &lt;5,000 non-N bases or &lt;20,000 bp in length and with &lt;10,000 non-N bases were removed. Pseudomolecules were generated with 10,000 Ns separating the scaffold sequences and were oriented and numbered in accordance with previously published maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Shirasawa, K. et al. Integrated consensus map of cultivated peanut and wild relatives reveals structures of the A and B genomes of Arachis and divergence of the legume genomes. DNA Res. 20, 173–184 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3008">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gautami, B. et al. An international reference consensus genetic map with 897 marker loci based on 11 mapping populations for tetraploid groundnut (Arachis hypogaea L.). PLoS One 7, e41213 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3011">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A microsatellite-based, gene-rich linkage map for the AA genome of Arachis (Fabaceae). Theor. Appl. Genet. 111, 1060–1071 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3014">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Moretzsohn, M.C. et al. A linkage map for the B-genome of Arachis (Fabaceae) and its synteny to the A-genome. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 40 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3017">24</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Characterization of transposons.</h3><p>Mobile elements were identified using a number of homology and <i>de novo</i> structural pattern finding algorithms and manual curation. For details, see the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p><i>Estimation of transposon coverages.</i> All annotated transposons were combined together and used as a library to screen the diploid genomes (pseudomolecules and unplaced scaffolds) using RepeatMasker with default parameters except with -nolow and -norna to not mask low-complexity sequences and rDNA, respectively. The output files were summarized using a custom Perl script, and regions masked by more than one sequence in the repeat library were recognized and counted only once. Base-pair counts for the diploid genomes excluded gaps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene prediction and annotation.</h3><p>Genome assemblies were masked with RepeatMasker using the repeat libraries developed for the two diploid species and annotated for gene models using the MAKER-P pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Campbell, M.S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3048">62</a></sup>. <i>Arachis</i>-specific models for the <i>ab initio</i> gene predictor SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3058">63</a></sup> were trained using high-scoring gene models from a first iteration of the pipeline and then used in the final annotation pass; no training was done for the other <i>ab initio</i> predictors included in the pipeline. RNA sequencing <i>de novo</i> assemblies for <i>A. hypogaea</i> and the diploid <i>Arachis</i> species were supplied as transcript evidence along with available EST and mRNA data sets from NCBI for these same species. Further evidence was supplied by proteomes derived from the annotations for <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. vulgaris</i> and <i>Medicago truncatula</i> as represented in Phytozome v. 10 (ref. 64). Default MAKER-P parameters were used for all other options, with the exception of disabling splice isoform prediction (alt_splice = 0) and forcing start and stop codons into every gene (always_complete = 1). The resulting MAKER-P gene models were post-processed to exclude from the main annotation files gene models with relatively poor support (annotation evidence distance scores of ≥0.75) or with significant BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421–430 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3085">64</a></sup> homology to identified mobile elements (HSP (high-scoring segment pair) coverage over ≥50% of the transcript sequence at ≥80% identity and <i>e</i> value ≤1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>). Provisional functional assignments for the gene models were produced using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3094">65</a></sup> and BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Hallab, A. Protein Function Prediction Using Phylogenomics, Domain Architecture Analysis, Data Integration, and Lexical Scoring. PhD thesis, Univ. Bonn (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3098">66</a></sup> against annotated proteins from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>G. max</i> and <i>M. truncatula</i>, with outputs processed using AHRD (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>), for lexical analysis and selection of the best functional descriptor of each gene product.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Analysis of gene duplications.</h3><p>The protein sequences of precalculated gene families were downloaded from Phytozome 10. Multiple-sequence alignments were built for each gene family using Muscle<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Edgar, R.C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792–1797 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3127">67</a></sup>. HMMs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Eddy, S.R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3131">68</a></sup> were built from each gene family alignment and were searched against the protein sequences of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> using HMMER. Genes were assigned family IDs using their best hits. Local gene duplication was defined as genes from the same gene family within ten successive genes and was calculated by a sliding window with a window size of ten genes and a step of one gene. After the calculation, only the number of locally duplicated genes was recorded.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">DNA methylation analysis. See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Note</a>.</h3><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Disease resistances and NB-LRR–encoding genes.</h3><p>We used two complementary approaches to identify R-gene candidates: HMM scans (HMMER 3.1b1; ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Eddy, S.R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3158">68</a>) against the Pfam protein domains TIR, NB-ARC and nine LRRs plus the <i>Arabidopsis</i> NB-ARC domain (NBS_712) to provide information about domain composition and a BLASTP search<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3165">69</a></sup> against two consensus sequences for the TIR and non-TIR classes of the NB-ARC–encoding genes to assist in distinguishing the two classes (NBS-TIR and NBS-CC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Ameline-Torregrosa, C. et al. Identification and characterization of nucleotide-binding site–leucine-rich repeat genes in the model plant Medicago truncatula. Plant Physiol. 146, 5–21 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3169">70</a></sup>. Results were compiled in Microsoft Excel for further analysis, and candidates that matched with an expectation value better than 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> were considered significant (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM224">Supplementary Data Set 6</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Gene evolution in <i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i> and species divergence.</h3><p>All-by-all synteny and <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> comparisons were made between <i>A. duranensis</i>, <i>A. ipaensis</i>, <i>G. max</i>, <i>Lotus japonicus</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>. Synteny blocks were identified within and between these species using DAGchainer, on the basis of gene alignments. <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values for aligned genes were calculated using the codeml method from the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3221">71</a></sup>. Median values were then taken for each synteny block. On the basis of known WGD and speciation information, the structure of a phylogenetic tree for species of interest was constructed, with branch lengths derived from modal <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> values from the <i>K</i><sub>S</sub> plots. Internal branch lengths were calculated from a system of equations based on modal values from all species comparisons.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Analysis of chromosomal structure and synteny.</h3><p>Structural comparisons between <i>A. ipaensis</i> and <i>A. duranensis</i> were made using visual interpretations of dot plots created using mummer and mummerplot from the MUMmer suite of alignment tools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3248">72</a></sup>. Gene density plots were created using CViT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Cannon, E.K. &amp; Cannon, S.B. Chromosome visualization tool: a whole genome viewer. Int. J. Plant Genomics 2011, 373875 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3252">73</a></sup>. Synteny comparisons were also made with other legume genomes (<i>G. max</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i>, <i>M. truncatula</i> and <i>P. vulgaris</i>), using MUMmer and DAGChainer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Haas, B.J., Delcher, A.L., Wortman, J.R. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. DAGchainer: a tool for mining segmental genome duplications and synteny. Bioinformatics 20, 3643–3646 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3269">74</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Sequence comparisons to tetraploid cultivated peanut.</h3><p>Moleculo reads were mapped against the diploid chromosomal pseudomolecules using nucmermaxgap = 500 -mincluster = 100. Show-coords, a nucmer utility, was run on the resulting nucmer delta files to produce alignment files. A single 'best' alignment to the diploid pseudomolecules was selected for each <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner Moleculo read. Alignment selection was based primarily on length and secondarily on identity. Show-tiling, another nucmer utility, was used to produce the tiling path of Moleculo reads. Output files were further processed using in-house scripts and Microsoft Excel (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM227">Supplementary Data Set 9</a>).</p><p><i>Analysis of genetic exchange in cultivated peanut RILs.</i> Paired-end sequence reads of restriction site–associated DNA sequencing for 166 RILs and their parents were obtained from the Sequence Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR1236437">SRR1236437</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRR1236438">SRR1236438</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zhou, X. et al. Construction of a SNP-based genetic linkage map in cultivated peanut based on large scale marker development using next-generation double-digest restriction-site-associated DNA sequencing (ddRADseq). BMC Genomics 15, 351 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3306">41</a></sup>. The data were divided into 168 subsets of individuals (2 parents and 166 RILs) on the basis of index tags. Low-quality sequences (quality value of &lt;10) and adaptors (AGATCGGAAGAGC) were trimmed with PRINSEQ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Schmieder, R. &amp; Edwards, R. Quality control and preprocessing of metagenomic datasets. Bioinformatics 27, 863–864 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3310">75</a></sup> and fastx_clipper in the FASTX-Toolkit. The filtered reads were mapped onto the two <i>Arachis</i> diploid genomes with Bowtie 2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="R Development Core Team. R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3317">76</a>) (parameters of --minins 0 --maxins 1000). The resultant SAM files excluding reads mapped at multiple loci on the reference were converted to BAM files with SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3321">60</a></sup>. Depth of coverage in 1-Mb bins was calculated from the BAM files with the GenomeCoverageBed option in BEDtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Quinlan, A.R. &amp; Hall, I.M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841–842 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3325">77</a></sup> (using parameter -d). Biases with depth of coverage among the RILs due to different numbers of mapped reads were corrected by converting the depth of coverage into the percentage relative to the total number of mapped reads in each line. Log<sub>2</sub>-transformed ratios of the corrected values in each RIL to that in the parent were calculated and plotted with R (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="R Development Core Team. R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2014)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3330">76</a>) and Excel.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Generation and assembly of transcribed sequences.</h3><p>Details of the tissues sampled can be found in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Table 12</a> and the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p><i>Sequencing of cDNAs.</i> Libraries were constructed with Illumina TruSeq RNA Sample Preparation v2 kit (tissues listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">Supplementary Tables 12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3517#MOESM218">13</a>). Three biological replicates were used for diploid tissue, and five biological replicates were used for <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner tissues (for some seed stages, only two biological replicates were used). For diploids, RNA from biological replicates was pooled before generating the libraries. For tetraploids, libraries constructed individually for each biological replicate were combined in equimolar pools for sequencing. Libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 instrument with a total of 209 cycles of TruSeq Rapid SBS kit v1 (Illumina) chemistry. For the diploids, to obtain longer reads to improve transcriptome assemblies, size-selected libraries were sequenced using an Illumina MiSeq instrument with v3 chemistry, and additional paired-end sequencing data were generated on Illumina NextSeq500.</p><p><i>Quality control and k-mer normalization.</i> Total raw reads were trimmed 10 bp from the 5′ end and 2 bp from the 3′ end after inspection of nucleotide bias using FastQC. Trimmed reads were then aligned to a compiled set of rRNA sequences using Bowtie, allowing two mismatches per 25-bp seed, and mapped reads were discarded. rRNA contamination varied from as low as 0.39% to as high as 51%, but most libraries had between 1–2% rRNA contamination. A total of 2,064,268,316 paired-end reads (4,128,536,632 total 100-nt reads) were subjected to <i>k</i>-mer normalization using the Trinity package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Grabherr, M.G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3370">78</a></sup>. A script was used to randomly discard reads with mean <i>k</i>-mer coverage of more than 20.</p><p><i>Transcriptome assemblies.</i> Adaptor and quality trimming was performed using Trim Galore! v0.3.5. Transcripts were assembled using the genome-guided pipeline from Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Haas, B.J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494–1512 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3382">79</a></sup>. For <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i>, reads were mapped to their respective genomes using GSNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Wu, T.D. &amp; Nacu, S. Fast and SNP-tolerant detection of complex variants and splicing in short reads. Bioinformatics 26, 873–881 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3392">80</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C.N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3395">81</a></sup>. For <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner, a diploid genome–guided tetraploid assembly was carried out: total reads were mapped to an <i>in silico</i> tetraploid genome (a concatenate of the chromosomal pseudomolecules of <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A ipaensis</i>). Once the reads were mapped, the SAM files were run through the genome-guided pipeline. Briefly, loci of reads were extracted into separate directories, where they were then assembled on a locus-by-locus basis. For the <i>A. hypogaea</i> cv. Tifrunner assembly, information on whether loci were guided by <i>A. duranensis</i> or <i>A. ipaensis</i> was retained so that transcripts were annotated as being either 'A' or 'B'.</p><p><i>Expression-based filtering of the final assembly of tetraploid transcripts.</i> Total reads were mapped to the transcript assembly from the 58 libraries using Bowtie, allowing two mismatches in a 25-bp seed. Fragments per kilobase per million reads mapped (FPKM) values were estimated using RSEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wu, T.D. &amp; Watanabe, C.K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3427">82</a></sup> for each library. When reads map to multiple transcripts, RSEM fractionates the read count among the transcripts such that read counts are not integers. Transcripts were filtered out that had FPKM &lt;1 for all 58 libraries using filter_fasta_by_rsem_values.pl from the Trinity package and were deemed lacking in minimum read coverage evidence to be supported. Expression-based filtered transcripts were tested for redundancy using a custom script to retain locus information from the assembly in the transcript names. Filtering was performed using an intra-subgenome self-BLAST. Transcripts with 90% or greater coverage and 100% identity were filtered out, leaving the longer transcript. Transcripts were aligned to the annotated repetitive sequences from <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> using GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Wu, T.D. &amp; Watanabe, C.K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859–1875 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3517#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d23641e3437">82</a></sup>, with the following parameters: -n 4 where -n controls the number of paths.</p><p><i>Estimation of the accuracy of transcriptome assembly of A. hypogaea cv. Tifrunner using diagnostic sequences. A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> pseudomolecules were fragmented into 100-bp fragments that were mapped to their opposite genome using Bowtie. The SAM files were filtered for fragments that mapped uniquely and completely (no clipping) with a maximum of only one mismatch to the opposite genome. These fragments were collected as diagnostic sequences. To test the accuracy of the assembled transcripts, diagnostic sequences were mapped to the transcript assembly using GSNAP with the following parameters: -n 1 -m 0 -A sam --nofails. Fragments diagnostic for A and mapping to A-derived transcripts were counted as correct, and those mapping to B-derived transcripts were counted as ambiguous. This testing was also done for B diagnostic transcripts.</p><p><i>Diploid protein comparisons.</i> Assembled transcripts were compared to the combined <i>A. duranensis</i> and <i>A. ipaensis</i> predicted protein models using BLASTX (<i>e</i> value &lt; 1 × 10<sup>−20</sup>), and the best hit was taken for each. Using AWK, sets of hits were filtered for the following criteria: &gt;80% amino acid identity and &gt;70% coverage of the protein model; &gt;80% amino acid identity and &gt;80% coverage of the protein model; 90% amino acid identity and &gt;80% coverage of the protein model; and &gt;90% amino acid identity and &gt;90% coverage of the protein model.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">URLs.</h3><p>Food and Agriculture Organization Corporate Statistical Database (FAOSTAT), <a href="http://faostat3.fao.org/home/">http://faostat3.fao.org/home/</a>; MadMapper, <a href="http://www.atgc.org/XLinkage/MadMapper/">http://www.atgc.org/XLinkage/MadMapper/</a>; RepeatMasker, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>; FastQC, <a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/">http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/</a>; Trim Galore! v0.3.5, <a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/trim_galore/">http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/trim_galore/</a>; FASTX-Toolkit, <a href="http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/">http://hannonlab.cshl.edu/fastx_toolkit/</a>; PeanutBase, <i>Arachis</i> genome sequences and repeat libraries, <a href="http://peanutbase.org/download">http://peanutbase.org/download</a>; Natural Earth maps, <a href="http://www.naturalearthdata.com/">http://www.naturalearthdata.com/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Accession codes.</h3><p>Genome assemblies and annotations, identified transposable elements, transcript assemblies and map data are available at <a href="http://www.peanutbase.org/download">http://www.peanutbase.org/download</a>. Genome assemblies have also been deposited in GenBank under assembly accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000817695.1/">GCA_000817695.1</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/assembly/GCA_000816755.1/">GCA_000816755.1</a>. MethylC-seq data are available under Gene Expression Omnibus (GEO) accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/geo/query/acc.cgi?acc=GSE71357">GSE71357</a>.</p></div></div></section>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;dopt=Abstract&amp;list_uids=23131301" aria-label="View reference 5 on PubMed" rel="nofollow">PubMed</a>
